"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_pair"" title=""Electron pair"">electron pair</a>"|"
Two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrons</span></a> which occupy the same <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#molecular_orbital""><span title=""See entry on this page at § molecular orbital"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">molecular orbital</span></a> but have opposite <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin_(physics)"" title=""Spin (physics)"">spins</a>. Electron pairs form <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#bond""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bond"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical bonds</span></a> or occur as <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#lone_pair""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lone pair"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lone pairs</span></a> of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#valence_electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § valence electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">valence electrons</span></a>; it is also possible for electrons to occur individually as <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#unpaired_electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § unpaired electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">unpaired electrons</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_shell"" title=""Electron shell"">electron shell</a>"|"
An <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atomic_orbital""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atomic orbital"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">orbital</span></a> around the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#nucleus""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nucleus"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nucleus</span></a> of an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atom</span></a> which contains a fixed number of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrons</span></a> (usually two or eight).
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronegativity"" title=""Electronegativity"">electronegativity</a> (<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chi_(letter)"" title=""Chi (letter)"">χ</a>)"|"
A chemical property that describes the tendency of an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atom</span></a> to attract a shared <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron_pair""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron pair"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pair</span></a> of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrons</span></a> (or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron_density""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron density"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electron density</span></a>) towards itself. An atom's electronegativity is affected both by its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#effective_nuclear_charge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § effective nuclear charge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nuclear charge</span></a> (which is proportional to the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atomic_number""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atomic number"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">number of protons</span></a> in its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#nucleus""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nucleus"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nucleus</span></a>) and the number and location of the electrons present in its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron_shell""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron shell"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atomic shells</span></a> (which influences the distance of the nucleus from the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#valence_electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § valence electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">valence electrons</span></a>). The higher an atom or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#substituent""><span title=""See entry on this page at § substituent"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">substituent</span></a>'s electronegativity, the more it attracts electrons towards itself. As it is usually calculated, electronegativity is not a property of an atom alone but rather of an atom within a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#molecule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § molecule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">molecule</span></a>; it therefore varies with an element's chemical environment, though it is generally considered a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#transferability""><span title=""See entry on this page at § transferability"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">transferable property</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrophile"" title=""Electrophile"">electrophile</a>"|"
Any atom or molecule which can accept an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron_pair""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron pair"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electron pair</span></a>. Most electrophiles carry a net <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electric_charge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric charge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">positive charge</span></a>, include an atom carrying a partial positive charge, or include a neutral atom that does not have a complete <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#octet_rule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § octet rule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">octet</span></a> of electrons, and therefore they attract electron-rich regions of other species; an electrophile with vacant orbitals can accept an electron pair donated by a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#nucleophile""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nucleophile"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nucleophile</span></a>, creating a chemical bond between the two species. Because they accept electrons, electrophiles are <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#lewis_acid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Lewis acid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Lewis acids</span></a> by definition.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrosynthesis"" title=""Electrosynthesis"">electrosynthesis</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_element"" title=""Chemical element"">element</a>"|"
A species of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atoms</span></a> having the same number of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#proton""><span title=""See entry on this page at § proton"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">protons</span></a> in their <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#nucleus""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nucleus"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atomic nuclei</span></a> and hence the same <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atomic_number""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atomic number"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atomic number</span></a>. Chemical elements constitute all of the ordinary <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#matter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § matter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">matter</span></a> in the universe; 118 elements have been identified and are organized by their various chemical properties in the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#periodic_table""><span title=""See entry on this page at § periodic table"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">periodic table of the elements</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elementary_reaction"" title=""Elementary reaction"">elementary reaction</a>"|"
Any <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#chemical_reaction""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chemical reaction"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical reaction</span></a> in which one or more <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#chemical_species""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chemical species"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical species</span></a> react directly to form <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#product""><span title=""See entry on this page at § product"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">products</span></a> in a single <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#reaction_step""><span title=""See entry on this page at § reaction step"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">reaction step</span></a> and with a single <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#transition_state""><span title=""See entry on this page at § transition state"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">transition state</span></a>, i.e. without any <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#reactive_intermediate""><span title=""See entry on this page at § reactive intermediate"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">intermediates</span></a>. Contrast <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#stepwise_reaction""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stepwise reaction"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stepwise reaction</span></a></i>.
"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Energy"" title=""Energy"">energy</a>"|"
A system's ability to do <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#work""><span title=""See entry on this page at § work"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">work</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amount_of_substance"" title=""Amount of substance"">enplethy</a>"|"
See <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#amount_of_substance""><span title=""See entry on this page at § amount of substance"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">amount of substance</span></a></i>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enthalpy"" title=""Enthalpy"">enthalpy</a>"|"
A measure of the total <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internal_energy"" title=""Internal energy"">internal energy</a> of a thermodynamic system, usually symbolized by <i>H</i>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enthalpy_of_fusion"" title=""Enthalpy of fusion"">enthalpy of fusion</a>"|"

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entropy"" title=""Entropy"">entropy</a>"|"
The amount of energy not available for <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#work""><span title=""See entry on this page at § work"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">work</span></a> in a closed thermodynamic system, usually symbolized by <i>S</i>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmental_chemistry"" title=""Environmental chemistry"">environmental chemistry</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enzyme"" title=""Enzyme"">enzyme</a>"|"
A biological <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein"" title=""Protein"">protein</a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#catalyst""><span title=""See entry on this page at § catalyst"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">catalyst</span></a> that speeds up a chemical reaction.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empirical_formula"" title=""Empirical formula"">empirical formula</a>"|"
Gives the simplest whole-number ratio of the atoms of each element present in a compound.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_types_of_equilibrium"" title=""List of types of equilibrium"">equilibrium</a>"|"
Universally, it is the condition of a system in which all competing influences are balanced. <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_equilibrium"" title=""Chemical equilibrium"">Chemical equilibrium</a> is the state in which the concentrations of the reactants and products have stopped changing in time.
"
"Eppendorf tube"|"
A generalized and trademarked name used to refer to a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#microcentrifuge_tube""><span title=""See entry on this page at § microcentrifuge tube"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">microcentrifuge tube</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erlenmeyer_flask"" title=""Erlenmeyer flask"">Erlenmeyer flask</a>"|"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intensive_and_extensive_properties"" title=""Intensive and extensive properties"">extensive property</a>"|"
A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_quantity"" title=""Physical quantity"">physical quantity</a> whose value is proportional to the size of the system it describes or to the quantity of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#matter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § matter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">matter</span></a> in the system. Examples include <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#mass""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mass"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mass</span></a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#volume""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volume"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">volume</span></a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#enthalpy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § enthalpy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">enthalpy</span></a>, and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#entropy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § entropy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">entropy</span></a>. Contrast <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#intensive_property""><span title=""See entry on this page at § intensive property"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">intensive property</span></a></i>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extraction_(chemistry)"" title=""Extraction (chemistry)"">extraction</a>"|"

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freezing"" title=""Freezing"">freezing</a>"|"
The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#phase_transition""><span title=""See entry on this page at § phase transition"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">phase transition</span></a> of a substance from a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#liquid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § liquid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">liquid</span></a> to a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#solid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § solid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">solid</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faraday_constant"" title=""Faraday constant"">Faraday constant</a>"|"
A unit of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electric_charge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric charge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric charge</span></a> widely used in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electrochemistry""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electrochemistry"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrochemistry</span></a> which represents 1&#160;<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#mole""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mole"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mole</span></a> of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrons</span></a>: 6.022&#160;×&#160;10<sup>23</sup> electrons. It is equal to approximately&#160;96,500&#160;<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#coulomb""><span title=""See entry on this page at § coulomb"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">coulombs</span></a> (<i>F</i> = 96&#160;485.339 9(24) C/mol).
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faraday%27s_laws_of_electrolysis"" title=""Faraday&#39;s laws of electrolysis"">Faraday's laws of electrolysis</a>"|"
A set of two laws pertaining to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrolysis"" title=""Electrolysis"">electrolysis</a> which hold that: a) the mass of a substance altered at an electrode during electrolysis is directly proportional to the quantity of electricity transferred at that electrode; and b) the mass of an elemental material altered at an electrode is directly proportional to the element's equivalent weight.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fick%27s_laws_of_diffusion"" title=""Fick&#39;s laws of diffusion"">Fick's laws of diffusion</a>"|"

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Filtration"" title=""Filtration"">filtration</a>"|"
Any physical, biological, or chemical operation that separates large particles (often <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#solid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § solid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">solid</span></a> matter) from smaller particles (often a fluid) by passing the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#mixture""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mixture"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mixture</span></a> through a complex lattice structure through which only particles of a sufficiently small size can pass, called a <i>filter</i>. The fluid and small particles which successfully pass through the filter are called the <i>filtrate</i>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rate_equation#First_order_reactions"" title=""Rate equation"">first-order reaction</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laboratory_flask"" title=""Laboratory flask"">flask</a>"|"
A vessel or container, most commonly a type of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glassware"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Glassware"">glassware</a>, widely used in laboratories for a variety of purposes, such as preparing, holding, containing, collecting, or volumetrically measuring chemicals, samples, or solutions, or as a chamber in which a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#chemical_reaction""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chemical reaction"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical reaction</span></a> occurs. Flasks come in a number of shapes and sizes but are typically characterized by a wider vessel ""body"" and one or more narrower tubular sections with an opening at the top.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formal_charge"" title=""Formal charge"">formal charge</a> (FC)"|"
The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electric_charge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric charge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric charge</span></a> assigned to an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atom</span></a> in a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#molecule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § molecule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">molecule</span></a>, assuming that all <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrons</span></a> in all <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#chemical_bond""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chemical bond"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bonds</span></a> are shared equally between atoms, regardless of each atom's relative <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electronegativity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electronegativity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electronegativity</span></a>. The formal charge of any atom that is part of a molecule can be calculated by the equation <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle FC=V-N-{\frac {B}{2}}\ }"">

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freezing_point"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Freezing point"">freezing point</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also <b>crystallization point</b>.</span></p>
The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a> at which a substance changes <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#state_of_matter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § state of matter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">state</span></a> from a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#liquid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § liquid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">liquid</span></a> to a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#solid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § solid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">solid</span></a>. Because <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#freezing""><span title=""See entry on this page at § freezing"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">freezing</span></a> is the reverse of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#melting""><span title=""See entry on this page at § melting"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">melting</span></a>, the freezing point of a substance is identical to its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#melting_point""><span title=""See entry on this page at § melting point"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">melting point</span></a>, but by convention only the melting point is referred to as a characteristic property of a substance.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frequency"" title=""Frequency"">frequency</a>"|"
A measurement of the number of cycles of a given process per unit of time. The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#sI""><span title=""See entry on this page at § SI"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">SI</span></a> unit for measuring frequency is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hertz"" title=""Hertz"">hertz</a> (Hz), with 1 Hz = 1 cycle per second.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Functional_group"" title=""Functional group"">functional group</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galvanic_cell"" title=""Galvanic cell"">galvanic cell</a>"|"
A type of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battery_(electricity)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Battery (electricity)"">battery</a> made up of electrochemicals with two different metals connected by a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#salt_bridge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § salt bridge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">salt bridge</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gas"" title=""Gas"">gas</a>"|"
One of the four fundamental <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#state_of_matter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § state of matter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">states of matter</span></a>, characterized by high-energy particles which fill their container but have no definite shape or volume.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gas_chromatography"" title=""Gas chromatography"">gas chromatography</a>"|"
A type of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#chromatography""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chromatography"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chromatography</span></a> commonly used in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#analytical_chemistry""><span title=""See entry on this page at § analytical chemistry"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">analytical chemistry</span></a> to isolate and analyze chemical compounds that can be <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#evaporation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § evaporation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">vaporized</span></a> without <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#chemical_decomposition""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chemical decomposition"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">decomposition</span></a>. Gas chromatography is often used to test the purity of substances, to identify unknown substances, and to measure the relative amounts of the different components of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#mixture""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mixture"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mixtures</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gay-Lussac%27s_law"" title=""Gay-Lussac&#39;s law"">Gay-Lussac's law</a>"|"
A chemical law used for each of the two relationships derived by French chemist <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac"" title=""Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac"">Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac</a> and which concern the properties of gases, though the name is more usually applied to his law of combining volumes.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geochemistry"" title=""Geochemistry"">geochemistry</a>"|"
The study of the chemistry and chemical composition of the Earth and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geology"" title=""Geology"">geological</a> processes.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gibbs_energy"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Gibbs energy"">Gibbs energy</a>"|"
A value that indicates the spontaneity of a reaction. Usually symbolized as <i>G</i>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glass"" title=""Glass"">glass</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gram-atom"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Gram-atom"">gram-atom</a>"|"
One gram-atom of an element is defined as a collection of 6.023X10^23 atoms.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grignard_reaction"" title=""Grignard reaction"">Grignard reaction</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ground_glass_joint"" title=""Ground glass joint"">ground glass joint</a>"|"
An apparatus designed to quickly and easily fit two pieces of leak-tight <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glassware"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Glassware"">glassware</a> together, featuring <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ground_glass"" title=""Ground glass"">ground glass</a> surfaces and typically a custom-made conical taper.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_(periodic_table)"" title=""Group (periodic table)"">group</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also <b>family</b>.</span></p>
A vertical column of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#periodic_table""><span title=""See entry on this page at § periodic table"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">periodic table of the elements</span></a> and the elements that share it. Contrast <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#period""><span title=""See entry on this page at § period"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">period</span></a></i>."
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halogen"" title=""Halogen"">halogen</a>"|"
Any of the five <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#non-metal""><span title=""See entry on this page at § non-metal"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">non-metallic</span></a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#element""><span title=""See entry on this page at § element"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elements</span></a> of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#group""><span title=""See entry on this page at § group"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Group 17</span></a> of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#periodic_table""><span title=""See entry on this page at § periodic table"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">periodic table</span></a>: <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluorine"" title=""Fluorine"">fluorine</a> (F), <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chlorine"" title=""Chlorine"">chlorine</a> (Cl), <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bromine"" title=""Bromine"">bromine</a> (Br), <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iodine"" title=""Iodine"">iodine</a> (I), and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astatine"" title=""Astatine"">astatine</a> (At)./dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadron"" title=""Hadron"">hadron</a>"|"
A subatomic particle of a type including the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baryon"" title=""Baryon"">baryons</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meson"" title=""Meson"">mesons</a> that can take part in the strong interaction.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat"" title=""Heat"">heat</a>"|"
Energy transferred from one system to another by thermal interaction.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enthalpy_of_fusion"" title=""Enthalpy of fusion"">heat of fusion</a>"|"
See <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#enthalpy_of_fusion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § enthalpy of fusion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">enthalpy of fusion</span></a></i>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry%27s_law"" title=""Henry&#39;s law"">Henry's law</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hess%27_law_of_constant_heat_summation"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Hess&#39; law of constant heat summation"">Hess' law of constant heat summation</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also simply called <b>Hess' law</b>.</span></p>
A law of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#physical_chemistry""><span title=""See entry on this page at § physical chemistry"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">physical chemistry</span></a> which states that the total <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#enthalpy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § enthalpy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">enthalpy</span></a> change during the course of a chemical reaction is the same whether the reaction is completed in one step or in multiple steps.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hund%27s_rules"" title=""Hund&#39;s rules"">Hund's rules</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrate"" title=""Hydrate"">hydrate</a>"|"
Any substance that contains <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water"" title=""Water"">water</a> or its constituent elements, or any <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#compound""><span title=""See entry on this page at § compound"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">compound</span></a> formed by the addition of water or its elements to another molecule.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydration_reaction"" title=""Hydration reaction"">hydration reaction</a>"|"

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_bond"" title=""Hydrogen bond"">hydrogen bond</a>"|"
A form of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrostatic_force"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Electrostatic force"">electrostatic interaction</a> between an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electronegativity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electronegativity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electronegative</span></a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atom</span></a> and a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#hydrogen""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hydrogen"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hydrogen</span></a> atom bound to a second electronegative atom. Hydrogen bonding is unique because the small size of the hydrogen atoms permits proximity of the interacting <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electrical_charge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electrical charge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrical charges</span></a>, and may occur as an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#intermolecular_force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § intermolecular force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">intermolecular</span></a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#intramolecular_force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § intramolecular force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">intramolecular</span></a> force.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogenation"" title=""Hydrogenation"">hydrogenation</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrolysis"" title=""Hydrolysis"">hydrolysis</a>"|"
The cleavage of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#bond""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bond"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical bond</span></a> by the addition of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#water""><span title=""See entry on this page at § water"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">water</span></a>.
"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gas_constant"" title=""Gas constant"">ideal gas constant</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also <b>universal gas constant</b>.</span></p>
The proportionality constant in the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#ideal_gas_law""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ideal gas law"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ideal gas law</span></a>, defined as 0.08206 <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#liter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § liter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">L</span></a>·<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atmosphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atmosphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atm</span></a>/(<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#kelvin""><span title=""See entry on this page at § kelvin"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">K</span></a>·<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#mole""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mole"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mol</span></a>).
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_gas_law"" title=""Ideal gas law"">ideal gas law</a>"|"
A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#chemical_law""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chemical law"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical law</span></a> which states that the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#volume""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volume"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">volume</span></a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#gas""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gas"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gas</span></a> is proportional to the amount of gas and its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#kelvin""><span title=""See entry on this page at § kelvin"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Kelvin</span></a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a> and inversely proportional to its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#pressure""><span title=""See entry on this page at § pressure"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pressure</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_solution"" title=""Ideal solution"">ideal solution</a>"|"
A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#solution""><span title=""See entry on this page at § solution"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">solution</span></a> for which the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#gas""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gas"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gas</span></a> phase exhibits thermodynamic properties analogous to those of a mixture of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#ideal_gas""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ideal gas"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ideal gases</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dependent_and_independent_variables"" title=""Dependent and independent variables"">independent variable</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PH_indicator"" title=""PH indicator"">indicator</a>"|"
A special compound added to a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#solution""><span title=""See entry on this page at § solution"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">solution</span></a> that changes color depending on the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#acidity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § acidity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">acidity</span></a> of the solution. Different indicators have different colors and are effective within different <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#pH""><span title=""See entry on this page at § pH"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pH</span></a> ranges.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Induced_radioactivity"" title=""Induced radioactivity"">induced radioactivity</a>"|"
radioactivity caused by bombarding a stable isotope with elemental particles, forming a radioactive isotope
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemically_inert"" title=""Chemically inert"">inert</a>"|"

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inorganic_compound"" title=""Inorganic compound"">inorganic compound</a>"|"
Any chemical compound that does not contain <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#carbon""><span title=""See entry on this page at § carbon"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">carbon</span></a>, though there are exceptions. Contrast <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#organic_compound""><span title=""See entry on this page at § organic compound"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">organic compound</span></a></i>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inorganic_chemistry"" title=""Inorganic chemistry"">inorganic chemistry</a>"|"
The branch of chemistry concerning the chemical properties and reactions of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#inorganic_compound""><span title=""See entry on this page at § inorganic compound"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">inorganic compounds</span></a>. Contrast <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#organic_chemistry""><span title=""See entry on this page at § organic chemistry"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">organic chemistry</span></a></i>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insulator_(electrical)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Insulator (electrical)"">insulator</a>"|"
Any material that resists the flow of an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electric_current""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric current"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric current</span></a>. Contrast <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#conductor""><span title=""See entry on this page at § conductor"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">conductor</span></a></i>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intensive_and_extensive_properties"" title=""Intensive and extensive properties"">intensive property</a>"|"
A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_quantity"" title=""Physical quantity"">physical quantity</a> whose value does not depend on the size of the system or the quantity of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#matter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § matter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">matter</span></a> for which it is measured. Examples include <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#density""><span title=""See entry on this page at § density"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">density</span></a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a>, and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#pressure""><span title=""See entry on this page at § pressure"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pressure</span></a>. Contrast <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#extensive_property""><span title=""See entry on this page at § extensive property"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">extensive property</span></a></i>.
"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry"" title=""International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry"">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC)"|"
An international federation of chemists that is recognized as the world authority in developing standards for <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#chemical_nomenclature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chemical nomenclature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical nomenclature</span></a> and other methodologies in chemistry.

<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ion"" title=""Ion"">ion</a>"|"
A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#molecule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § molecule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">molecule</span></a> that has gained or lost one or more <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrons</span></a> from its neutral state and therefore possesses a negative or positive <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electric_charge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric charge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric charge</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionic_bond"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Ionic bond"">ionic bond</a>"|"
An electrostatic attraction between oppositely charged <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#ion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ions</span></a>.
<div class=""thumb tright""><div class=""thumbinner"" style=""width:222px;""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NaF.gif"" class=""image""><img alt="""" src=""//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NaF.gif/220px-NaF.gif"" decoding=""async"" width=""220"" height=""96"" class=""thumbimage"" srcset=""//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NaF.gif/330px-NaF.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NaF.gif/440px-NaF.gif 2x"" data-file-width=""560"" data-file-height=""245"" /></a>  <div class=""thumbcaption""><div class=""magnify""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NaF.gif"" class=""internal"" title=""Enlarge""></a></div>An <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#ionic_bond""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ionic bond"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ionic bond</span></a> between a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium"" title=""Sodium"">sodium</a> atom (Na) and a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluorine"" title=""Fluorine"">fluorine</a> atom (F). The sodium atom loses its sole <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valence_electron"" title=""Valence electron"">valence electron</a> (leaving the atom with a positive <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_charge"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Electrical charge"">electrical charge</a>), and the fluorine atom gains this same electron via an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exothermic_process"" title=""Exothermic process"">exothermic process</a> (giving the atom a negative electrical charge). The oppositely charged <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ion"" title=""Ion"">ions</a> are then attracted to each other to form a new compound called <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium_fluoride"" title=""Sodium fluoride"">sodium fluoride</a>.</div></div></div>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionization"" title=""Ionization"">ionization</a>"|"
The breaking up of a chemical <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#compound""><span title=""See entry on this page at § compound"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">compound</span></a> into separate ions.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isoelectronicity"" title=""Isoelectronicity"">isoelectronicity</a>"|"
The phenomenon of two or more <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#chemical_species""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chemical species"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical species</span></a> (<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atoms</span></a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#molecule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § molecule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">molecules</span></a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#ion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ions</span></a>, etc.) being composed of different <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#element""><span title=""See entry on this page at § element"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elements</span></a> but having the same number of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#valence_electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § valence electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">valence</span></a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrons</span></a> and the same structural arrangement (i.e. the same number of atoms with the same connectivity). Isoelectronic species typically show useful consistency and predictability in their chemical properties.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isomerization"" title=""Isomerization"">isomerization</a>"|"
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Chemistry-Terms-Flashcards"">project</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms"">""Glossary of chemistry terms""</a> by making a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isomer"" title=""Isomer"">isomers</a>"|"
<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#ion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Ions</span></a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#molecule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § molecule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">molecules</span></a> with identical <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#chemical_formula""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chemical formula"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical formulas</span></a> but distinct structures or spatial arrangements. Isomers do not necessarily share similar properties. The two main types of isomers are <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#structural_isomer""><span title=""See entry on this page at § structural isomer"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">structural isomers</span></a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#stereoisomer""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stereoisomer"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stereoisomers</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotope"" title=""Isotope"">isotope</a>"|"
A variant of a particular <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#element""><span title=""See entry on this page at § element"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical element</span></a> which differs in the number of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#neutron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § neutron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">neutrons</span></a> present in the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#nucleus""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nucleus"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nucleus</span></a>. All isotopes of a given element have the same number of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#proton""><span title=""See entry on this page at § proton"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">protons</span></a> in each <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atom</span></a>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joule"" title=""Joule"">joule</a> (J)"|"
The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#sI""><span title=""See entry on this page at § SI"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">SI</span></a> unit of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy</span></a> (symbol: <i>J</i>). One joule is defined as one <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newton_(unit)"" title=""Newton (unit)"">Newton</a>-<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meter"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Meter"">meter</a>."
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kelvin"" title=""Kelvin"">kelvin</a> (K)"|"
The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#sI""><span title=""See entry on this page at § SI"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">SI</span></a> base unit of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a> (symbol: <i>K</i>). The Kelvin scale is an absolute <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature"" title=""Thermodynamic temperature"">thermodynamic temperature</a> scale that uses <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#absolute_zero""><span title=""See entry on this page at § absolute zero"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">absolute zero</span></a> as its null point.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ketone"" title=""Ketone"">ketone</a>"|"
An organic compound with a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#carbonyl""><span title=""See entry on this page at § carbonyl"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">carbonyl</span></a> group between two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#carbon""><span title=""See entry on this page at § carbon"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">carbon</span></a> atoms.
<div class=""thumb tright""><div class=""thumbinner"" style=""width:222px;""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ketone-group-2D-skeletal.svg"" class=""image""><img alt="""" src=""//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ketone-group-2D-skeletal.svg/220px-Ketone-group-2D-skeletal.svg.png"" decoding=""async"" width=""220"" height=""220"" class=""thumbimage"" srcset=""//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ketone-group-2D-skeletal.svg/330px-Ketone-group-2D-skeletal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ketone-group-2D-skeletal.svg/440px-Ketone-group-2D-skeletal.svg.png 2x"" data-file-width=""425"" data-file-height=""425"" /></a>  <div class=""thumbcaption""><div class=""magnify""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ketone-group-2D-skeletal.svg"" class=""internal"" title=""Enlarge""></a></div>The skeletal formula for a generic <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#ketone""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ketone"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ketone</span></a>, with <i>R</i> and <i>R'</i> denoting variable carbon-containing substituent groups</div></div></div>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_kinetics"" title=""Chemical kinetics"">kinetics</a>"|"
A subfield of chemistry specializing in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#reaction_rate""><span title=""See entry on this page at § reaction rate"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">reaction rates</span></a>.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kinetic_energy"" title=""Kinetic energy"">kinetic energy</a>"|"
The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy</span></a> of an object due to its motion."
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lability"" title=""Lability"">lability</a>"|"

The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#period""><span title=""See entry on this page at § period"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">periodic</span></a> series of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#metal""><span title=""See entry on this page at § metal"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">metallic</span></a> elements with atomic numbers 57 through 71, from <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lanthanum"" title=""Lanthanum"">lanthanum</a> through <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lutetium"" title=""Lutetium"">lutetium</a>.

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leveling_effect"" title=""Leveling effect"">leveling effect</a>"|"
The effect of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#solvent""><span title=""See entry on this page at § solvent"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">solvent</span></a> on the chemical properties of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#acid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § acid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">acids</span></a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#base""><span title=""See entry on this page at § base"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bases</span></a> which are dissolved in the solvent. The strength of a strong acid is limited or ""leveled"" by the basicity of the solvent, and likewise the strength of a strong base is limited by the acidity of the solvent, such that the effective <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#pH""><span title=""See entry on this page at § pH"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pH</span></a> of the solution is higher or lower than might be suggested by the acid's or base's <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#acid_dissociation_constant""><span title=""See entry on this page at § acid dissociation constant"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">dissociation constant</span></a>.
"

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ligand"" title=""Ligand"">ligand</a>"|"
An <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#ion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ion</span></a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#functional_group""><span title=""See entry on this page at § functional group"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">functional group</span></a>, or other <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#molecule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § molecule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">molecule</span></a> that binds to a central <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#metal""><span title=""See entry on this page at § metal"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">metal</span></a> atom to form a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#coordination_complex""><span title=""See entry on this page at § coordination complex"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">coordination complex</span></a>. Such bonding can range from <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#covalent_bond""><span title=""See entry on this page at § covalent bond"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">covalent</span></a> to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#ionic_bond""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ionic bond"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ionic</span></a>, but generally involves formal donation of one or more of the ligand's <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electron_pair""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron pair"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electron pairs</span></a> to the metal.
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light"" title=""Light"">light</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also referred to as <b>visible light</b>.</span></p>
The portion of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#electromagnetic_spectrum""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electromagnetic spectrum"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electromagnetic spectrum</span></a> which is visible to the unaided human eye.

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manometer"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Manometer"">manometer</a>"|"
An instrument used to measure <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#pressure""><span title=""See entry on this page at § pressure"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pressure</span></a> invented by <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evangelista_Torricelli"" title=""Evangelista Torricelli"">Evangelista Torricelli</a> in 1643.

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass"" title=""Mass"">mass</a>"|"
A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#physical_property""><span title=""See entry on this page at § physical property"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">property</span></a> of physical <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#matter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § matter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">matter</span></a> that is a measure of its resistance to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acceleration"" title=""Acceleration"">acceleration</a> when a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_force"" title=""Net force"">net force</a> is applied. The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_chemistry_terms#sI""><span title=""See entry on this page at § SI"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">SI</span></a> base unit for mass is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilogram"" title=""Kilogram"">kilogram</a> (kg).
